   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Holocene extinction</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
      Jump to: 
     <a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 
     <a href="#p-search">search</a> 
    </div>

         <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, became extinct during the mid-late seventeenth century after 
         <a href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans" class="mw-redirect">humans</a> destroyed the 
         <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forests</a> where the birds made their homes and introduced mammals that ate their eggs.

        </div> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The <b>Holocene extinction</b>, otherwise referred to as the <b>Sixth extinction</b> or <b>Anthropocene extinction</b>, is a name for the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">extinction event</a> of <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> during the present <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(geology)" title="Epoch (geology)">epoch</a> (since around 10,000 BCE) mainly due to human activity. The large number of extinctions span numerous families of <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> including <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>. Although 875 extinctions occurring between 1500 and 2009 have been documented by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature_and_Natural_Resources" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources" class="mw-redirect">International Union for Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> with widespread degradation of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_hotspot" title="Biodiversity hotspot">highly biodiverse</a> habitats such as <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a>, as well as other areas, the vast majority are thought to be undocumented. According to the <a href="/wiki/Species-area_curve" title="Species-area curve">species-area theory</a> and based on upper-bound estimating, the present rate of extinction may be up to 140,000 species per year,<sup id="cite_ref-:1_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> making it the greatest loss of biodiversity since the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>.</p> 
     <p>The Holocene extinction includes the disappearance of large land animals known as <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a>, starting between 9,000 and 13,000 years ago, the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last Ice Age</a>. Megafauna outside of the African continent, that did not evolve alongside humans, proved highly sensitive to the introduction of new predation, and many died out shortly after early humans began spreading and hunting across the Earth (additionally, many <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of African animals extinct in the Holocene">African species have also gone extinct in the Holocene</a>). The extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoths</a>, whose habits had maintained <a href="/wiki/Grasslands" title="Grasslands" class="mw-redirect">grasslands</a> became birch forests without them.<sup id="cite_ref-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> The new forest and the resulting forest fires may have induced <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> Such disappearances might be the result of the <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">proliferation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Modern_human" title="Modern human" class="mw-redirect">modern humans</a>. These extinctions, occurring near the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>–<a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> boundary, are sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">Quaternary extinction event</a>.</p> 
     <p>There is no general agreement on where the Holocene, or <a href="/wiki/Anthropogenic" title="Anthropogenic" class="mw-redirect">anthropogenic</a>, extinction begins, and the Quaternary extinction event which includes climate change resulting in the end of the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a> ends, or if they should be considered separate events at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Some have suggested that anthropogenic extinctions may have begun as early as when the first modern humans spread out of Africa between 100,000 and 200,000 years ago, which is supported by rapid megafaunal extinction following recent human colonisation in <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_New_Zealand" title="List of extinct animals of New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of African animals extinct in the Holocene">Madagascar</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> in a similar way that any <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">large, adaptable predator moving into a new ecosystem would</a>. In many cases, it is suggested even minimal hunting pressure was enough to wipe out large fauna, particularly on <a href="/wiki/Geographical_isolation" title="Geographical isolation" class="mw-redirect">geographically isolated</a> islands.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Only during the most recent parts of the extinction have <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_climate_change_on_plant_biodiversity" title="Effect of climate change on plant biodiversity">plants also suffered large losses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The ecology of <i>Homo sapiens</i> has been noted as being that of an unprecedented 'global superpredator' that regularly preys on the adults of other <a href="/wiki/Apex_predators" title="Apex predators" class="mw-redirect">apex predators</a> and has worldwide effects on food webs.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Extinctions of species have occurred on every land mass and ocean, with many famous examples <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of African animals extinct in the Holocene">within Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Asian_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of Asian animals extinct in the Holocene">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Europe" title="List of extinct animals of Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Australia" title="List of extinct animals of Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_North_American_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of North American animals extinct in the Holocene">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_South_American_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of South American animals extinct in the Holocene">South America</a>, and on smaller islands. Overall, the Holocene extinction can be characterized by the <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human impact on the environment</a>. The Holocene extinction continues into the 21st century, with <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_amphibian_populations" title="Decline in amphibian populations">amphibian crisis</a> being a few broader examples of an almost universal, cosmopolitan decline of biodiversity.</p> 
     <p>It has been suggested human activity has made the period following the mid-20th century different enough from the Holocene to consider it a new <a href="/wiki/Geological_epoch" title="Geological epoch" class="mw-redirect">geological epoch</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> which will be considered for implementation into the timeline of Earth's history by the <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_on_Stratigraphy" title="International Commission on Stratigraphy">International Commission on Stratigraphy</a> in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p></p>

       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Contemporary_crises"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Contemporary crises</span></a> 
        <ul> 
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Amphibian_crisis"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Amphibian crisis</span></a></li> 
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Mass_bat_deaths"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Mass bat deaths</span></a></li> 
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Global_warming"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Global warming</span></a></li> 
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Degradation_of_marine_habitats"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Degradation of marine habitats</span></a></li> 
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Pollinator_decline"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">Pollinator decline</span></a></li> 
        </ul> </li> 
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Lists_of_affected_species"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Lists of affected species</span></a></li> 
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li> 
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>

      </ul> 
     </div> 
     <p></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_influence_on_extinction">Human influence on extinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Human influence on extinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:443px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Extinctions_Africa_Austrailia_NAmerica_Madagascar.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Extinctions_Africa_Austrailia_NAmerica_Madagascar.gif" width="441" height="452" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="441" data-file-height="452"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        The&nbsp;% of 
        <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> on different land masses over time, with the arrival of humans indicated.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Extinction of animals, plants, and other organisms caused by human actions may go as far back as the late <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, over 12,000 years ago. There is evidence that abrupt climate change has especially played an enormous role in the extinction of larger mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> However, while previous mass extinctions were due to natural environmental causes, research shows that wherever on Earth humans have migrated, other species have gone extinct, and <a href="/wiki/Human_population_growth" title="Human population growth" class="mw-redirect">human population growth</a>, most prominently in the past two centuries, is regarded as one of the underlying causes of extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> In terms of how humans have contributed, three major factors include the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Anthropogenic_greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gas">increased global concentration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>, affecting the global climate; oceanic devastation, such as through <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> and contamination; and the modification and destruction of vast tracts of land and river systems around the world to meet solely human-centered ends (with 10 to 15 percent of Earth's land surface now used as urban-industrial or row-crop agricultural sites and 6 to 8 percent used as pastures), thus ruining the local ecosystems.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Other, related human causes of the extinction event include <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> the introduction in various regions of <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">non-native species</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_disease" title="Globalization and disease">widespread</a> <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(medicine)" title="Transmission (medicine)">transmission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease" class="mw-redirect">infectious diseases</a>. At present, the rate of extinction of species is estimated at 100 to 1,000 times higher than the "base" or historically typical rate of extinction (in terms of the natural evolution of the planet)<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> and also the current rate of extinction is, therefore, 10 to 100 times higher than any of the previous <a href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction" class="mw-redirect">mass extinctions</a> in the history of Earth. It is also the only known mass extinction of plants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>The abundance of species extinctions considered <i><a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a></i>, or due to human activity, have sometimes (especially when referring to hypothesized future events) been collectively called the "Anthropocene extinction".<sup id="cite_ref-wooldridge2008_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wooldridge2008-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jackson2008_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jackson2008-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a> is a term introduced in 2000. It is now widely accepted that a new geological epoch has begun, characterised by the most abrupt and widespread extinction of species since the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> 66 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> In <i>The Future of Life</i> (2002), <a href="/wiki/E.O._Wilson" title="E.O. Wilson" class="mw-redirect">E.O. Wilson</a> of Harvard calculated that, if the current rate of human disruption of the biosphere continues, one-half of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s higher lifeforms will be extinct by 2100. A 1998 poll conducted by the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a> found that seventy percent of biologists believe that we are in the midst of an anthropogenic extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Numerous scientific studies—such as a 2004 report published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> and papers authored by the 10,000 scientists who contribute to the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" title="IUCN" class="mw-redirect">IUCN</a>'s annual <a href="/wiki/Red_List" title="Red List" class="mw-redirect">Red List</a> of threatened species—have since reinforced this conviction.</p> 
     <p>The evidence of all previous extinctions is geological in nature, and shorter geological time scale is of the order of several hundred thousand to several million years. Even extinctions caused by instantaneous events such as the impact of the <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_impactor" title="Chicxulub impactor">asteroid in Chicxulub</a>, which is currently the best example, extend the equivalent of many human lives, due to complex ecological interactions that were triggered by the event.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>Recent extinctions described are well-documented,<sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature" title="Nomenclature">nomenclature</a> used varies. The term <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a> is a term that is used by few scientists,<sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> and some commentators may refer to the current and projected future extinctions as part of a longer Holocene extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> The Holocene–Anthropocene boundary is contested, with some commentators asserting significant human influence on climate for much of what is normally regarded as the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(geology)" title="Epoch (geology)">Epoch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Other commentators place the Holocene–Anthropocene boundary at the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution" class="mw-redirect">industrial revolution</a> while also saying that "Formal adoption of this term in the near future will largely depend on its utility, particularly to earth scientists working on late Holocene successions."<sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Three hypotheses have been proposed to explain the extinction of <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">late Pleistocene</a>. Of these, only two have much scientific credibility. Although Ross McPhee proposed that a hyper-disease may have been the cause of the extinction,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> a study by Lyons <i>et al.</i>, demonstrated conclusively that a hyperdisease was unlikely to have caused the extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> The two main theories to the extinction are <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> and <a href="/wiki/Overkill_hypothesis" title="Overkill hypothesis" class="mw-redirect">human hunting</a>. The climate change theory has suggested that a change in climate near the end of the late Pleistocene stressed the megafauna to the point of extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Some scientists favor abrupt climate change as the catalyst for the extinction of the mega-fauna at the end of the Pleistocene, but there are many who believe increased hunting from early modern humans also played a part, with others even suggesting that the two interacted.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> In the Americas, a controversial explanation for the shift in climate is presented under the <a href="/wiki/Younger_Dryas_impact_hypothesis" title="Younger Dryas impact hypothesis">Younger Dryas impact hypothesis</a>.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">Megafauna</a> was once found on every continent of the world and large islands such as <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, but is now almost exclusively found on the continent of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, with notable comparisons on <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australia</a> and the islands previously mentioned experiences population crashes and <a href="/wiki/Trophic_cascade" title="Trophic cascade">trophic cascades</a> shortly after the earliest human settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> It has been suggested that the African megafauna survived as they evolved alongside humans.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> The timing of <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene_megafauna" title="Pleistocene megafauna">South American megafaunal</a> extinction does not appear to correspond to human arrival, although the possibility of whether human activity at the time may have impacted the global climate enough to cause such an extinction has been suggested.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>It has been noted, in the face of such evidence, <i>Homo sapiens</i> is unique in its ecology as an unprecedented 'global superpredator', regularly preying on large numbers of fully grown terrestrial and marine <a href="/wiki/Apex_predators" title="Apex predators" class="mw-redirect">apex predators</a>, and with a great deal of influence over food webs and climatic systems worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_extinctions">Prehistoric extinctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Prehistoric extinctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <td class="mbox-image"> 
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="262" data-file-height="204"></a>
         </div> </td> 
        <td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(October 2012)</i></small></span></td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Megafauna#Megafaunal_mass_extinctions" title="Megafauna">Megafaunal mass extinctions</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">Quaternary extinction event</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Although significant debate exists as to how much human predation and indirect effects contributed to prehistoric extinctions, certain population crashes have been directly correlated with human arrival.<sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australian megafauna</a>, 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Australia" title="List of extinct animals of Australia">List of extinct animals of Australia</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_flora_of_Australia" title="List of extinct flora of Australia">List of extinct flora of Australia</a>
     </div> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species_in_Australia" title="Invasive species in Australia">Invasive species in Australia</a>, 
      <a href="/wiki/Land_clearing_in_Australia" title="Land clearing in Australia">Land clearing in Australia</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">Fire-stick farming</a>
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> was once home to a <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">large assemblage of megafauna</a>, with many parallels to those found on the African continent today. Australia's fauna is characterised by primarily <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">marsupial</a> mammals, and many reptiles and birds, all existing as giant forms until recently. <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Humans</a> arrived on the continent very early, about 50,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> The extent human arrival contributed is controversial; climatic drying of Australia 40,000-60,000 years ago was an unlikely cause, as it was less severe in speed or magnitude than previous regional climate change which failed to kill off megafauna.</p> 
     <p>Due to the older timeframe and the soil chemistry on the continent, very little <a href="/wiki/Subfossil" title="Subfossil">subfossil</a> preservation evidence exists than elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> However, continent-wide extinction of all genera weighing over 100 kilograms, and six of seven genera weighing between 45 and 100 kilograms occurred around 46,400 years ago (4,000 years after human arrival)<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> and the fact that megafauna survived until a later date on the island of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a> following the establishment of a land bridge<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> suggest direct hunting or anthropogenic ecosystem disruption such as <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">fire-stick farming</a> as likely causes. The first evidence of direct human predation leading to extinction in Australia was published in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Genyornis_BW.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Genyornis_BW.jpg/220px-Genyornis_BW.jpg" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Genyornis_BW.jpg/330px-Genyornis_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Genyornis_BW.jpg/440px-Genyornis_BW.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1002"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Genyornis_BW.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Genyornis_newtoni" title="Genyornis newtoni" class="mw-redirect">Genyornis newtoni</a></i>, a 7 foot tall flightless bird. Evidence of egg cooking in this species is the first evidence of megafaunal hunting by humans on Australia.
        <sup id="cite_ref-:7_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <ul> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Dromornithidae" title="Dromornithidae">Dromornithidae</a>,</i> an entire family of giant birds. Though around the size of <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emus</a>, they are actually more related to ducks and other <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a>. Analysis of burned eggshells in <i><a href="/wiki/Genyornis_newtoni" title="Genyornis newtoni" class="mw-redirect">Genyornis newtoni</a></i>, which stood around 7 feet tall, suggested human consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> As of yet, this is the most reliable direct evidence of human predation on Australian megafauna.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Diprotodon" title="Diprotodon">Diprotodon</a>,</i> giant relatives of <a href="/wiki/Wombat" title="Wombat">wombats</a></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Zygomaturus" title="Zygomaturus">Zygomaturus</a>,</i> a marsupial superficially resembling a <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros" title="Rhinoceros">rhinoceros</a></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Hulitherium" title="Hulitherium">Hulitherium</a>,</i> a large marsupial herbivore</li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Phascolonus" title="Phascolonus">Phascolonus</a>,</i> a giant wombat</li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Palorchestes" title="Palorchestes">Palorchestes</a> azael,</i> a marsupial "<a href="/wiki/Tapir" title="Tapir">tapir</a>"</li> 
      <li>A highly diverse assemblage of <a href="/wiki/Kangaroo" title="Kangaroo">kangaroos</a>. These include <a href="/wiki/Island_gigantism" title="Island gigantism">giant</a> species from several genus, including <i><a href="/wiki/Macropus_titan" title="Macropus titan">Macropus titan</a>,</i> <i><a href="/wiki/Simosthenurus" title="Simosthenurus">Simosthenurus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sthenurus" title="Sthenurus">Sthenurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Protemnodon" title="Protemnodon">Protemnodon</a>.</i> Other unique kangaroo radiations include <i><a href="/wiki/Procoptodon" title="Procoptodon">Procoptodon goliah</a>,</i> a hoof-toed giant short-faced kangaroo, and <i><a href="/wiki/Propleopus" title="Propleopus">Propleopus oscillans</a>,</i> an omnivorous kangaroo</li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Wonambi" title="Wonambi">Wonambi</a>,</i> a five-to-six-metre-long Australian constrictor <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snake</a></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Thylacoleo_carnifex" title="Thylacoleo carnifex" class="mw-redirect">Thylacoleo carnifex</a>,</i> a lioness-sized marsupial carnivore</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Megalania" title="Megalania"><i>Varanus priscus,</i></a> a giant predatory monitor lizard</li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Zaglossus_hacketti" title="Zaglossus hacketti">Zaglossus hacketti</a>,</i> a giant species of <a href="/wiki/Echidna" title="Echidna">echidna</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Quinkana" title="Quinkana">Quinkana</a>, a five-to-six metre-long predatory terrestrial crocodile</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>Extinctions in Australia continued from original settlement until today in both <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_flora_of_Australia" title="List of extinct flora of Australia">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Australia" title="List of extinct animals of Australia">animals</a>, whilst <a href="/wiki/Threatened_fauna_of_Australia" title="Threatened fauna of Australia">many more animals</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_threatened_flora_of_Australia" title="List of threatened flora of Australia">plants</a> have declined or are endangered.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_and_South_America">North and South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: North and South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_North_American_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of North American animals extinct in the Holocene">List of North American animals extinct in the Holocene</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_South_American_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of South American animals extinct in the Holocene">List of South American animals extinct in the Holocene</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Mammoth_House_(Replica).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG/220px-Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG/330px-Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG/440px-Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3842" data-file-height="2252"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Mammoth_House_(Replica).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Reconstructed 
        <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoth</a> bone hut, based on finds in 
        <a href="/wiki/Mezhyrich" title="Mezhyrich">Mezhyrich</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>There has been a debate as to the extent to which the disappearance of <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> at the end of the last <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">glacial period</a> can be attributed to human activities by hunting, or even by slaughter<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> of prey populations. Discoveries at Monte Verde in South America and at <a href="/wiki/Meadowcroft_Rock_Shelter" title="Meadowcroft Rock Shelter" class="mw-redirect">Meadowcroft Rock Shelter</a> in Pennsylvania have caused a controversy<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> regarding the <a href="/wiki/Clovis_culture" title="Clovis culture">Clovis culture</a>. There likely would have been human settlements prior to the Clovis Culture, and the history of <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">humans in the Americas</a> may extend back many thousands of years before the Clovis culture.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> The amount of correlation between human arrival and megafauna extinction is still being debated: for example, in Wrangel Island in Siberia the extinction of dwarf <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoths</a> (approximately 2000 BCE)<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> did not coincide with the arrival of humans, nor did megafaunal mass extinction on the South American continent, although it has been suggested climate changes induced by anthropogenic effects elsewhere in the world may have contributed.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Comparisons are sometimes made between recent extinctions (approximately since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution" class="mw-redirect">industrial revolution</a>) and the Pleistocene extinction near the end of the last <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">glacial period</a>. The latter is exemplified by the extinction of large herbivores such as the <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoth</a> and the carnivores that preyed on them. We know that humans of this era actively hunted the <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mastodon" title="Mastodon">mastodon</a><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> but it is not known if this hunting was the cause of the subsequent massive ecological changes, widespread extinctions and climate changes.<sup id="cite_ref-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The ecosystems encountered by the first Americans had not been exposed to human interaction, and may have been far less resilient to human made changes than the ecosystems encountered by industrial era humans. Therefore, the actions of the Clovis people, despite seeming insignificant by today's standards could indeed have had a profound effect on the ecosystems and wild life which was entirely unused to human influence.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Smilodon_fatalis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Smilodon_fatalis.jpg/220px-Smilodon_fatalis.jpg" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Smilodon_fatalis.jpg/330px-Smilodon_fatalis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Smilodon_fatalis.jpg/440px-Smilodon_fatalis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1559" data-file-height="900"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Smilodon_fatalis.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Smilodon_fatalis" title="Smilodon fatalis" class="mw-redirect">Smilodon fatalis</a></i>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The following species, among many others, became extinct in this period.</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Doedicurus" title="Doedicurus">Doedicurus</a>,</i> a giant <a href="/wiki/Armadillo" title="Armadillo">armadillo</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/American_cheetah" title="American cheetah">American cheetah</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/American_lion" title="American lion">American lion</a></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Holochilus_primigenus" title="Holochilus primigenus" class="mw-redirect">Holochilus primigenus</a></i></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Pygmy_mammoth" title="Pygmy mammoth">Pygmy mammoth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoth</a> and the local <a href="/wiki/Columbian_mammoth" title="Columbian mammoth">Columbian mammoth</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Mastodon" title="Mastodon">Mastodons</a></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Eremotherium" title="Eremotherium">Eremotherium</a></i></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Smilodon" title="Smilodon">Smilodon</a></i>, sabre toothed cats</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Teratorns" title="Teratorns" class="mw-redirect">Teratorns</a>, a group of birds that include some of the largest flying animals of all time</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean">Caribbean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Caribbean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Human arrival around 6,000 years ago is correlated with the extinction of many species.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Examples include:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Many different genera of <a href="/wiki/Pilosans_of_the_Caribbean" title="Pilosans of the Caribbean">ground and arboreal sloths across all islands</a>. These sloths were generally smaller than those found on the South American continent. <i><a href="/wiki/Megalocnus" title="Megalocnus">Megalocnus</a></i> were the largest genus at up to 90&nbsp;kg, <i><a href="/wiki/Acratocnus" title="Acratocnus">Acratocnus</a></i> were medium-sized relatives of modern <a href="/wiki/Two-toed_sloth" title="Two-toed sloth">two-toed sloths</a> endemic to <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Imagocnus" title="Imagocnus">Imagocnus</a></i> also of Cuba, <i><a href="/wiki/Neocnus" title="Neocnus">Neocnus</a></i> and many others.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Giant_hutia" title="Giant hutia">Giant hutias</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Cuban_coney" title="Cuban coney">Cuban coney</a></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Brotomys" title="Brotomys">Brotomys</a></i></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Cuban_giant_owl" title="Cuban giant owl" class="mw-redirect">Cuban giant owl</a></li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacific_islands">Pacific islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Pacific islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Oceania" title="List of extinct animals of Oceania">List of extinct animals of Oceania</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Recent research, based on <a href="/wiki/Archeology" title="Archeology" class="mw-redirect">archaeological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontological</a> digs on 70 different islands, has shown that numerous species went extinct as people moved across the Pacific, starting 30,000 years ago in the <a href="/wiki/Bismarck_Archipelago" title="Bismarck Archipelago">Bismarck Archipelago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> It is currently estimated that among the bird species of the Pacific some 2000 species have gone extinct since the arrival of humans, representing a 20% drop in the biodiversity of birds worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Among the extinctions were:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Sylviornis" title="Sylviornis">Sylviornis</a></i>, a giant <a href="/wiki/Galliformes" title="Galliformes">galliform</a> bird from <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_of_New_Caledonia" title="Biodiversity of New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Mekosuchine_crocodiles" title="Mekosuchine crocodiles" class="mw-redirect">Mekosuchine crocodiles</a> from New Caledonia, <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a>.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Meiolania" title="Meiolania">Meiolaniid turtles</a> on <a href="/wiki/Lord_Howe_Island" title="Lord Howe Island">Lord Howe Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Ikanogavialis" title="Ikanogavialis">"Gavialis" papuensis</a>, a marine crocodilian from the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a>.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Cretanelephant-petermaas.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Cretanelephant-petermaas.jpg/220px-Cretanelephant-petermaas.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fb/Cretanelephant-petermaas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fb/Cretanelephant-petermaas.jpg 2x" data-file-width="267" data-file-height="200"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Cretanelephant-petermaas.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Mammuthus_creticus" title="Mammuthus creticus">Mammuthus creticus</a></i>,a 
        <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_elephant" title="Dwarf elephant">dwarf elephant</a> once endemic to 
        <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>. It was one of many species of small elephants found on islands in the Mediterranean.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mediterranean_Islands">Mediterranean Islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mediterranean Islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <ul> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Pygmy_hippopotamus#Extinct_pygmy_and_dwarf_hippos" title="Pygmy hippopotamus">Pygmy hippos</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cyprus_dwarf_hippopotamus" title="Cyprus dwarf hippopotamus">Cyprus</a> (<i>Phanourios minutus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Cretan_dwarf_hippopotamus" title="Cretan dwarf hippopotamus" class="mw-redirect">Crete</a> (<i>Hippopotamus creutzburgi</i>), <a href="/wiki/Maltese_hippopotamus" title="Maltese hippopotamus" class="mw-redirect">Malta</a> (<i>H. melitensis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_hippopotamus" title="Sicilian hippopotamus" class="mw-redirect">Sicily</a> (<i>H. pentlandi</i>)</li> 
      <li>Balearic Islands cave goat (<i><a href="/wiki/Myotragus_balearicus" title="Myotragus balearicus" class="mw-redirect">Myotragus balearicus</a></i>) of <a href="/wiki/Majorca" title="Majorca">Majorca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minorca" title="Minorca">Minorca</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Dwarf_elephant" title="Dwarf elephant">A diverse assemblage of dwarf elephants</a> of several islands, including Cyprus (<i><a href="/wiki/Elephas_cypriotes" title="Elephas cypriotes" class="mw-redirect">Elephas cypriotes</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Elephas_falconeri" title="Elephas falconeri" class="mw-redirect">E. falconeri</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Mammuthus_creticus" title="Mammuthus creticus">Mammuthus creticus</a></i>)</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Giant_swan" title="Giant swan" class="mw-redirect">Giant swan</a> (<i>Cygnus falconeri</i>) of Malta</li> 
      <li>Giant dormice: <a href="/wiki/Minorcan_giant_dormouse" title="Minorcan giant dormouse">Minorcan giant dormouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Majorcan_giant_dormouse" title="Majorcan giant dormouse">Majorcan giant dormouse</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Sardinian_pika" title="Sardinian pika">Sardinian pika</a> of Sardinia and Corsica</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extinctions_into_the_Common_Era">Extinctions into the Common Era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Extinctions into the Common Era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>More recent settlement of isolated land masses where megafauna continued to survive almost immediately resulted in their extinction. Calculations suggest this occurred even if only a small number of animals were hunted. It has been suggested this contemporary evidence supports the theory that humans were capable of causing or at least contributing to the extinctions of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">Quaternary extinction event</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hawaiian_islands">Hawaiian islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hawaiian islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_the_Hawaiian_Islands" title="List of extinct animals of the Hawaiian Islands">List of extinct animals of the Hawaiian Islands</a>
     </div> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Endemism_in_the_Hawaiian_Islands" title="Endemism in the Hawaiian Islands">Endemism in the Hawaiian Islands</a>, 
      <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species_in_Hawaii" title="Invasive species in Hawaii">Invasive species in Hawaii</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_in_Hawaii" title="Environmental issues in Hawaii">Environmental issues in Hawaii</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Hawaii" title="Ancient Hawaii">first settlers</a> are thought to have arrived in the islands between 300 and 800 CE, with European arrival in the 16th century. Hawaii is notable for its <a href="/wiki/Endemism_in_the_Hawaiian_Islands" title="Endemism in the Hawaiian Islands">endemism</a> of plants, <a href="/wiki/Endemic_birds_of_Hawaii" title="Endemic birds of Hawaii">birds</a>, insects, mollusks and <a href="/wiki/List_of_fish_of_Hawaii" title="List of fish of Hawaii">fish</a>; 30% of its organisms are endemic. Many of its species are endangered or have gone extinct, primarily due to accidentally introduced species and livestock grazing. Over 40% of its bird species have gone extinct, and it is the location of 75% of extinctions in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Extinction has increased in Hawaii over the last 200 years and is relatively well documented, with extinctions among native snails used as estimates for global extinction rates.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>The <a href="/wiki/Moa-nalo" title="Moa-nalo">moa-nalos</a>, grazing ducks from <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>.</li> 
      <li>The <a href="/wiki/N%C4%93n%C4%93-nui" title="Nēnē-nui">nēnē-nui</a>, or woodwalking goose, a large species of goose that once inhabited the island of <a href="/wiki/Maui" title="Maui">Maui</a>.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Kaua%CA%BBi_finch" title="Kauaʻi finch">Kauaʻi finch</a>, a large <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">finch</a></li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_Ocean_Islands">Indian Ocean Islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Indian Ocean Islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Starting circa 1500 years ago, a number of species became extinct upon human settlement of the islands, including:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Several species of giant tortoise on the <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mascarene_Islands" title="Mascarene Islands">Mascarene Islands</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/Cylindraspis" title="Cylindraspis">Cylindraspis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Meiolania_platyceps" title="Meiolania platyceps" class="mw-redirect">Meiolania platyceps</a></i></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Aldabrachampsus" title="Aldabrachampsus">Aldabrachampsus</a>,</i> a Crocodyliform found on the <a href="/wiki/Aldabra_Atoll" title="Aldabra Atoll" class="mw-redirect">Aldabra Atoll</a></li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Madagascar">Madagascar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Madagascar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      Further information: 
      <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Madagascar" title="Wildlife of Madagascar">Wildlife of Madagascar</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Subfossil_lemur" title="Subfossil lemur">Subfossil lemur</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Megaladapis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Megaladapis.jpg/220px-Megaladapis.jpg" width="220" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Megaladapis.jpg/330px-Megaladapis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Megaladapis.jpg/440px-Megaladapis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="632" data-file-height="900"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">

        <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> recently wiped out by human activity on 
        <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:413px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg/411px-Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg.png" width="411" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg/617px-Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg/822px-Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="875" data-file-height="600"></a>
