        <a href="/wiki/Subfossil_lemur" title="Subfossil lemur">giant lemurs</a> were present in Madagascar until after human arrival.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Within 500 years of the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_people" title="Malagasy people">humans</a> between 2,500-2,000 years ago, nearly all of Madagascar's distinct, <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">geographically isolated</a> megafauna became extinct.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> The largest animals, of more than 150&nbsp;kg, were extincted very shortly after the first human arrival, with large and medium-sized species dying out after prolonged hunting pressure from an expanding human population moving into more remote regions of the island around 1000 years ago. Smaller fauna experienced initial increases due to decreased competition, and then subsequent declines over the last 500 years.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> All fauna weighing over 10&nbsp;kg died out. The primary reasons for this are human hunting and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Madagascar" title="Deforestation in Madagascar">habitat loss</a> from early <a href="/wiki/Aridification" title="Aridification">aridification</a>, both of which persist and threaten Madagascar's remaining taxa today.</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Eight or more species of <a href="/wiki/Elephant_bird" title="Elephant bird">elephant birds</a>, giant flightless <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a> in the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Aepyornis" title="Aepyornis">Aepyornis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mullerornis" title="Mullerornis">Mullerornis</a></i>.</li> 
      <li>17 species of lemur, known as giant, <a href="/wiki/Subfossil_lemur" title="Subfossil lemur">subfossil lemurs</a>. Some of these lemurs typically weighed over 150&nbsp;kg, and fossils have provided evidence of human butchery on many species.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> 
       <ul> 
        <li><a href="/wiki/Giant_aye-aye" title="Giant aye-aye">Giant aye-aye</a> (<i>Daubentonia robusta</i>)</li> 
        <li><a href="/wiki/Sloth_lemur" title="Sloth lemur">Sloth lemurs</a>, including chimpanzee-sized <i><a href="/wiki/Palaeopropithecus" title="Palaeopropithecus">Palaeopropithecus</a></i> and gorilla-sized <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeoindris" title="Archaeoindris">Archaeoindris</a></i></li> 
        <li><a href="/wiki/Koala_lemur" title="Koala lemur" class="mw-redirect">Koala lemurs</a> (<i>Megaladapis</i>), a koala-like, orangutan-sized arboreal lemur</li> 
        <li><a href="/wiki/Monkey_lemur" title="Monkey lemur">Monkey lemurs</a>, most terrestrial of lemurs, often compared to <a href="/wiki/Baboon" title="Baboon">baboons</a> or <a href="/wiki/Macaque" title="Macaque">macaques</a>.</li> 
        <li><i><a href="/wiki/Pachylemur" title="Pachylemur">Pachylemur</a></i>, a larger, more robust genus of <a href="/wiki/Ruffed_lemur" title="Ruffed lemur">ruffed lemurs</a></li> 
       </ul> </li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Giant_fossa" title="Giant fossa" class="mw-redirect">Giant fossa</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Voay" title="Voay">Voay</a>, a terrestrial giant <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_crocodile" title="Dwarf crocodile">dwarf crocodile</a></li> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Plesiorycteropus" title="Plesiorycteropus">Plesiorycteropus</a></i>, a genus containing two species of superficially <a href="/wiki/Aardvark" title="Aardvark">Aardvark</a>-like digging mammal unlike anything alive today</li> 
      <li>Three species of <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_hippopotamus" title="Malagasy hippopotamus">Malagasy hippopotamus</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Malagasy_crowned_eagle" title="Malagasy crowned eagle">Malagasy crowned eagle</a> (<i>Stephanoaetus mahery</i>), a giant bird of prey</li> 
      <li>Local <a href="/wiki/Giant_tortoises" title="Giant tortoises" class="mw-redirect">giant tortoises</a> of genus <i><a href="/wiki/Geochelone" title="Geochelone">Geochelone</a></i>, including <i><a href="/wiki/Aldabrachelys_abrupta" title="Aldabrachelys abrupta">Aldabrachelys abrupta</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Aldabrachelys_grandidieri" title="Aldabrachelys grandidieri">Aldabrachelys grandidieri</a></i>. The latter were important <a href="/wiki/Seed_dispersal" title="Seed dispersal">seed dispersers</a> for endemic <a href="/wiki/Baobab_tree" title="Baobab tree" class="mw-redirect">baobab trees</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
     </ul> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_extinctions_-_1500_onwards">Recent extinctions - 1500 onwards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Recent extinctions - 1500 onwards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>One scientist estimates the current extinction rate may be 10,000 times the <a href="/wiki/Background_extinction_rate" title="Background extinction rate">background extinction rate</a>. Nevertheless, most scientists predict a much lower extinction rate than this outlying estimate.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Pimm" title="Stuart Pimm">Stuart Pimm</a> stated "the current rate of species extinction is about 100 times the natural rate" for plants.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Mass extinctions are characterized by the loss of at least 75% of species within a geologically short period of time.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In a pair of studies published in 2015, extrapolation from observed extinction of Hawaiian snails led to the conclusion that 7% of all species on Earth may have been lost already.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Megafaunal extinctions continue into the 21st century. Modern extinctions are more directly attributable to human influences. Extinction rates are minimized in the popular imagination by the survival of captive populations of animals that are <i>extinct in the wild</i> (such as the <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A8re_David%27s_deer" title="Père David's deer">Père David's deer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_crow" title="Hawaiian crow">Hawaiian crow</a>) and by marginal survivals of highly publicized megafauna that are <i><a href="/wiki/Ecological_extinction" title="Ecological extinction">ecologically extinct</a></i> (such as the <a href="/wiki/Giant_panda" title="Giant panda">giant panda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumatran_rhinoceros" title="Sumatran rhinoceros">Sumatran rhinoceros</a>, North American <a href="/wiki/Black-footed_ferret" title="Black-footed ferret">black-footed ferret</a>). However, the Holocene can also be characterisied by widespread extinctions among <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, widespread <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction"><i>local</i> extinctions</a> of populations of species that still exist elsewhere (such as the extinction of <a href="/wiki/Gray_whale" title="Gray whale">gray whales</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Leatherback_sea_turtle" title="Leatherback sea turtle">leatherback sea turtle</a> in Malaysia) and by universal declines in range and population of various animal and plant species throughout all of the world.</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">IUCN</a> characterises 'recent' extinction as those that have occurred past the cut-off point of 1500. The extinct species listed below are not comprehensive, but are some of the most famous examples.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_New_Zealand" title="List of extinct animals of New Zealand">List of extinct animals of New Zealand</a>
     </div> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_of_New_Zealand" title="Biodiversity of New Zealand">Biodiversity of New Zealand</a>, 
      <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_New_Zealand_environment" title="Timeline of the New Zealand environment">Timeline of the New Zealand environment</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species_in_New_Zealand" title="Invasive species in New Zealand">Invasive species in New Zealand</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png/220px-Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png/330px-Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png/440px-Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png 2x" data-file-width="2285" data-file-height="1588"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> A size comparison between 4 recently extinct 
        <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> 
        <a href="/wiki/Moa" title="Moa">moa</a> species and a human. 
        <b>1.</b> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Dinornis_novaezealandiae" title="Dinornis novaezealandiae" class="mw-redirect">Dinornis novaezealandiae</a></i> 
        <b>2.</b> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Emeus_crassus" title="Emeus crassus" class="mw-redirect">Emeus crassus</a></i> 
        <b>3.</b> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Anomalopteryx_Didiformis" title="Anomalopteryx Didiformis" class="mw-redirect">Anomalopteryx didiformis</a></i> 
        <b>4.</b> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Dinornis_robustus" title="Dinornis robustus" class="mw-redirect">Dinornis robustus</a></i>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> is characterised by its <a href="/wiki/Geographic_isolation" title="Geographic isolation" class="mw-redirect">geographic isolation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insular_biogeography" title="Insular biogeography">island biogeography</a>, and had been isolated from mainland Australia for 80 million years. It was the last large land mass to be colonised by humans. The arrival of <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesian</a> settlers circa 12th century resulted in the extinction of all of the islands' megafaunal birds within several hundred years. The Polynesians also introduced kiore (rats) as a food source. This may have put some pressure on other birds but at the time of early European contact (18th Century) and colonisation (19th Century)the bird life was prolific. With them, the Europeans brought ship rats, possums, cats and mustelids which decimated native bird life, some of which had adapted <a href="/wiki/Flightless_bird" title="Flightless bird">flightlessness</a> and ground nesting habits and others had no defensive behavior as a result of having no extant <a href="/wiki/Endemic" title="Endemic" class="mw-redirect">endemic</a> mammalian predators. The Kakapo, the world's biggest parrot, which is flightless, now only exists in managed breeding sanctuaries and NZ's national emblem, the Kiwi, is on the endangered bird list.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Extinctions include, among <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_New_Zealand" title="List of extinct animals of New Zealand">many others</a>:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/List_of_moa" title="List of moa">Eleven species</a> of <a href="/wiki/Moa" title="Moa">moa</a>, giant flightless <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a>. The last moa went extinct around the 1500s, although accounts suggest some may have survived until more recently. It has been suggested even moderate hunting pressure was enough to drive moa across the islands to extinction in less than 200 years.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li>The giant <a href="/wiki/Haast%27s_eagle" title="Haast's eagle">Haast's eagle</a>, <i>Harpagornis</i></li> 
      <li>Two species of giant flightless predatory <a href="/wiki/Adzebill" title="Adzebill">adzebills</a>.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Finsch%27s_duck" title="Finsch's duck">Finsch's duck</a>, a large, flightless duck</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_notable_recent_extinctions">Other notable recent extinctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other notable recent extinctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_recently_extinct_mammals" title="List of recently extinct mammals">List of recently extinct mammals</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Panthera_tigris_sondaica_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Panthera_tigris_sondaica_01.jpg/220px-Panthera_tigris_sondaica_01.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Panthera_tigris_sondaica_01.jpg/330px-Panthera_tigris_sondaica_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Panthera_tigris_sondaica_01.jpg/440px-Panthera_tigris_sondaica_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1206" data-file-height="823"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Panthera_tigris_sondaica_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Javan_tiger" title="Javan tiger">Javan tiger</a> pictured 1938.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Some examples of modern extinctions of "charismatic" mammal fauna include:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><i><a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">Aurochs</a></i> (1627), a wild cow that was domesticated to form modern cattle.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_cow" title="Steller's sea cow">Steller's sea cow</a> (1768), a large <a href="/wiki/Sirenia" title="Sirenia">sirenian</a> driven to extinction across its prehistoric range across the North Pacific by hunting. The last populations, reduced to the <a href="/wiki/Commander_Islands" title="Commander Islands">Commander Islands</a>, were driven to extinction 27 years after their discovery by Europeans in 1741 (where they may have numbered at 2000) after heavy hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Sardinian_pika" title="Sardinian pika">Sardinian pika</a> (1774) primitive lagomorph native to the Mediterranean islands of Sardinia and Corsica</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Bluebuck" title="Bluebuck">Bluebuck</a>, a species of antelope. Despite already being uncommon by the time of European discovery, habitat conversion for agriculture and hunting resulted in its extinction by 1800.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Atlas_bear" title="Atlas bear">Atlas bear</a> (1870s)</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands_wolf" title="Falkland Islands wolf">Falkland Islands wolf</a> (1876)</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Quagga" title="Quagga">Quagga</a> (1883), zebra subspecies, Southeast Africa. Recent attempts have been made by the <a href="/wiki/Quagga_Project" title="Quagga Project">Quagga Project</a> to selectively breed <a href="/wiki/Burchell%27s_zebra" title="Burchell's zebra">Burchell's zebras</a> to superficially resemble quaggas.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Equus_ferus_ferus" title="Equus ferus ferus" class="mw-redirect">Tarpan</a>, a European wild horse. Although extinct in the wild by 1890, the last captive animal died in 1909.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Thylacine" title="Thylacine">Thylacine</a> (1936) or Tasmanian tiger, <i>Thylacinus cynocephalus</i>, a marsupial carnivore prosecuted by farmers and the government for predating on livestock.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Caribbean_monk_seal" title="Caribbean monk seal">Caribbean monk seal</a> (1950s)</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Japanese_sea_lion" title="Japanese sea lion">Japanese sea lion</a> (1970s)</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Pyrenean_ibex" title="Pyrenean ibex">Pyrenean ibex</a> (2000). Attempts were made to <a href="/wiki/De-extinction" title="De-extinction">clone the species</a> shortly after its extinction, and despite brief success the only surviving foetus died after 7 minutes.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Yangtze_dolphin" title="Yangtze dolphin" class="mw-redirect">Yangtze dolphin</a>, extinct 2006</li> 
      <li>The closely related <a href="/wiki/Bali_tiger" title="Bali tiger">Bali tiger</a> (1937) and <a href="/wiki/Javan_tiger" title="Javan tiger">Javan tiger</a> (1970s)</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_cougar" title="Eastern cougar">Eastern cougar</a> (2011)<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> a subspecies of <a href="/wiki/North_American_cougar" title="North American cougar">North American cougar</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Western_black_rhinoceros" title="Western black rhinoceros">Western black rhinoceros</a> (2011), a subspecies</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_recently_extinct_birds" title="List of recently extinct birds">List of recently extinct birds</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Po%CA%BBouli.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Po%CA%BBouli.jpg/220px-Po%CA%BBouli.jpg" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Po%CA%BBouli.jpg/330px-Po%CA%BBouli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Po%CA%BBouli.jpg/440px-Po%CA%BBouli.jpg 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="390"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Po%CA%BBouli.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Po%CA%BBouli" title="Poʻouli">Poʻouli</a>, a species of 
        <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_honeycreeper" title="Hawaiian honeycreeper">Hawaiian honeycreeper</a>, and the only member of its 
        <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a>. After severe habitat loss and predation from 
        <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species_in_Hawaii" title="Invasive species in Hawaii">invasive species</a> such as the 
        <a href="/wiki/Small_Asian_mongoose" title="Small Asian mongoose">small Asian mongoose</a> and feral 
        <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> and 
        <a href="/wiki/Rats" title="Rats" class="mw-redirect">rats</a>, the last individual died in 2004 after unsuccessful attempts at captive breeding.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Many birds have become extinct as a result of human activity, especially birds <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(ecology)" title="Endemic (ecology)" class="mw-redirect">endemic</a> to islands, including many flightless birds (<i>see a more complete list under</i> <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_birds" title="List of extinct birds" class="mw-redirect">extinct birds</a>). Notable extinct birds include:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Many species of <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_birds" title="List of extinct birds" class="mw-redirect">birds</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Mascarene_Islands" title="Mascarene Islands">Mascarene Islands</a>, including the giant flightless pigeons known as the <a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">dodo</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a> (1662) and the <a href="/wiki/Rodrigues_solitaire" title="Rodrigues solitaire">Rodrigues solitaire</a> (1778) and the unrelated <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union_sacred_ibis" title="Réunion sacred ibis" class="mw-redirect">Réunion solitaire</a>, which was a flightless <a href="/wiki/Ibis" title="Ibis">ibis</a> (early 1800s)</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Great_auk" title="Great auk">Great auk</a> (1852) once highly abundant flightless <a href="/wiki/Auk" title="Auk">auk</a> around the North Atlantic</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Passenger_pigeon" title="Passenger pigeon">Passenger pigeons</a> (1914) of North America, once so abundant that accounts suggest their flocks took days to pass</li> 
      <li>The <a href="/wiki/Carolina_parakeet" title="Carolina parakeet">Carolina parakeet</a> (1918) of the American southeast</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Ivory-billed_woodpecker" title="Ivory-billed woodpecker">Ivory-billed woodpecker</a> (2000)</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>Ten species or subspecies of birds have disappeared from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Hawaiian islands</a> since the 1980s, primarily due to <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species_in_Hawaii" title="Invasive species in Hawaii">invasive species</a> and habitat loss. These include:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>The entire <a href="/wiki/Mohoidae" title="Mohoidae">Mohoidae</a> <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">songbird</a> family, including <a href="/wiki/Moho_(genus)" title="Moho (genus)">four species of ʻōʻō</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Hawai%CA%BBi_%CA%BB%C5%8D%CA%BB%C5%8D" title="Hawaiʻi ʻōʻō">Hawaiʻi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kaua%CA%BBi_%CA%BB%C5%8D%CA%BB%C5%8D" title="Kauaʻi ʻōʻō">Kauaʻi ʻōʻōs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kioea" title="Kioea">kioea</a></li> 
      <li>All members of the group <a href="/wiki/Nukupu%E2%80%98u" title="Nukupu‘u">Nukupu‘u</a>. Two species went extinct in the 1990s.</li> 
      <li>All members of the <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">finch</a> genus <a href="/wiki/Akialoa" title="Akialoa">Akialoa</a>.</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/K%C4%81ma%CA%BBo" title="Kāmaʻo">Kāmaʻo</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Po%CA%BBouli" title="Poʻouli">Poʻouli</a>, a single species of <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_honeycreeper" title="Hawaiian honeycreeper">Hawaiian honeycreeper</a> within its own genus.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">Plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31"></a></td> 
        <td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section requires <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit">expansion</a>. <small><i>(February 2016)</i></small></span></td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_plants#Modern_extinctions" title="List of extinct plants">List of extinct plants</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Bufo_periglenes2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bufo_periglenes2.jpg/250px-Bufo_periglenes2.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bufo_periglenes2.jpg/375px-Bufo_periglenes2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bufo_periglenes2.jpg/500px-Bufo_periglenes2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3576" data-file-height="2385"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Bufo_periglenes2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Golden_toad" title="Golden toad">golden toad</a> of Costa Rica, extinct since around 1989. Its disappearance has been attributed to a confluence of several factors, including 
        <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> warming, fungus, and the introduction of invasive species.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_crises">Contemporary crises</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Contemporary crises">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List_extinct_in_the_wild_species" title="IUCN Red List extinct in the wild species">IUCN Red List extinct in the wild species</a>, 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_endangered_species" title="List of endangered species" class="mw-redirect">List of endangered species</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_critically_endangered_species" title="List of critically endangered species" class="mw-redirect">List of critically endangered species</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Peter Raven, past president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">American Association for the Advancement of Science</a> (AAAS), states in the foreword to their publication <i>AAAS Atlas of Population and Environment</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> "We have driven the rate of biological extinction, the permanent loss of species, up several hundred times beyond its historical levels, and are threatened with the loss of a majority of all species by the end of the 21st century."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>189 countries which are signatory to the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a> (Rio Accord) have committed to preparing a <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_Action_Plan" title="Biodiversity Action Plan" class="mw-redirect">Biodiversity Action Plan</a>, a first step at identifying specific <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a> and habitats, country by country.</p> 
     <p>Various species are predicted to <a href="/wiki/List_of_critically_endangered_species" title="List of critically endangered species" class="mw-redirect">go extinct in the near future</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amphibian_crisis">Amphibian crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Amphibian crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_amphibian_populations" title="Decline in amphibian populations">Decline in amphibian populations</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_amphibian_populations" title="Decline in amphibian populations">decline of amphibian populations</a> has also been identified as an indicator of environmental degradation. As well as habitat loss, introduced predators and pollution, <a href="/wiki/Chytridiomycosis" title="Chytridiomycosis">Chytridiomycosis</a>, a fungal infection thought to have been accidentally spread by human travel,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> has caused severe population drops of several species of frogs, including (among many others) the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_toad" title="Golden toad">golden toad</a> in Costa Rica and the <a href="/wiki/Gastric-brooding_frog" title="Gastric-brooding frog">Gastric-brooding frog</a> in Australia. Many other amphibian species now face extinction, including the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Rabb%27s_Fringe-limbed_Treefrog" title="Rabb's Fringe-limbed Treefrog" class="mw-redirect">Rabb's fringe-limbed treefrog</a> to an <a href="/wiki/Endling" title="Endling">endling</a>, and the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Panamanian_golden_frog" title="Panamanian golden frog">Panamanian golden frog</a> in the wild. Chytrid fungus has spread across Australia, New Zealand, Central America and Africa, including countries with high amphibian diversity such as <a href="/wiki/Cloud_forest" title="Cloud forest">cloud forests</a> in <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Batrachochytrium_salamandrivorans" title="Batrachochytrium salamandrivorans">Batrachochytrium salamandrivorans</a></i> is a similar infection currently threatening <a href="/wiki/Salamander" title="Salamander">salamanders</a>. Amphibians are now the most endangered vertebrate group, having existed for more than 300 million years through three other <a href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction" class="mw-redirect">mass extinctions</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_bat_deaths">Mass bat deaths</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Mass bat deaths">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/White_nose_syndrome" title="White nose syndrome">White nose syndrome</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Millions of bats in the US have been dying off since 2012 due to a fungal infection spread from European bats, which appear to be immune. Population drops have been as great as 90% within five years, and extinction of at least one bat species is predicted. There is currently no form of treatment, and such declines have been described as "unprecedented" in bat evolutionary history by Alan Hicks of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Department_of_Environmental_Conservation" title="New York State Department of Environmental Conservation">New York State Department of Environmental Conservation</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_warming">Global warming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Global warming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Extinction_risk_from_global_warming" title="Extinction risk from global warming">Extinction risk from global warming</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">Ocean acidification</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Global warming is widely accepted as being a contributor to extinction worldwide, in a similar way that <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">previous extinction events</a> have generally included a rapid change in global climate and meteorology. It is also expected to disrupt sex ratios in many reptiles which have <a href="/wiki/Temperature-dependent_sex_determination" title="Temperature-dependent sex determination">temperature-dependent sex determination</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Degradation_of_marine_habitats">Degradation of marine habitats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Degradation of marine habitats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">Ocean acidification</a>, 
      <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">Overfishing</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">Marine pollution</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:283px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg/281px-Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg" width="281" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg/422px-Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg/562px-Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="942" data-file-height="738"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The collapse of 
        <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">Atlantic cod</a> off the coast of Newfoundland in 1992 as a result of 
        <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>. The population never recovered, completely altering the ecosystem and rendering the species 
        <a href="/wiki/Functional_extinction" title="Functional extinction">locally extinct</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Rising levels of carbon dioxide are resulting in influx of this gas into the ocean, increasing its acidity. Marine organisms which possess Calcium Carbonate shells or exoskeletons experience physiological pressure as the carbonate reacts with acid. This is already resulting in <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">coral bleaching</a> on various <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> worldwide, which provide valuable habitat for very high biodiversity. Marine gastropods, bivalves and other invertebrates are also affected, as are any organisms that feed on them.</p> 
     <p>Fishing has had a devastating effect on marine organism populations for several centuries even before the explosion of destructive and highly effective fishing practices like <a href="/wiki/Trawling" title="Trawling">trawling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Humans are unique among predators in that they regularly predate on other adult <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predators</a>, particularly in marine environments;<sup id="cite_ref-:4_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> in particular are particularly vulnerable to predation pressure from human fishing.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollinator_decline">Pollinator decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Pollinator decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Pollinator_decline" title="Pollinator decline">Pollinator decline</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Colony_collapse_disorder" title="Colony collapse disorder">Colony collapse disorder</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The term <a href="/wiki/Pollinator_decline" title="Pollinator decline">pollinator decline</a> refers to the reduction in abundance of insect and other animal pollinators in many ecosystems worldwide beginning at the end of the twentieth century, and continuing into the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lists_of_affected_species">Lists of affected species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Lists of affected species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="reflist columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2; list-style-type: decimal;"> 
      <ol class="references"> 
       <li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.iucn.org/?4143/Extinction-crisis-continues-apace">"Extinction continues apace"</a>. International Union for Conservation of Nature. 3 November 2009<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 October</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHolocene+extinction&amp;rft.btitle=Extinction+continues+apace&amp;rft.date=2009-11-03&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iucn.org%2F%3F4143%2FExtinction-crisis-continues-apace&amp;rft.pub=International+Union+for+Conservation+of+Nature&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&nbsp;</span></span></span></li>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <ul> 
      <li><cite class="citation journal">deBuys, William (March 2015). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.tomdispatch.com/post/175968/tomgram%3A_william_debuys%2C_a_global_war_on_nature/">"The Politics of Extinction - A Global War on Nature"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Tom_Engelhardt" title="Tom Engelhardt">Tom Dispatch</a></i>. <q>Uncounted species -- not just tigers, gibbons, rhinos, and <a href="/wiki/Saola" title="Saola">saola</a>, but vast numbers of smaller mammals, amphibians, birds, and reptiles -- are being pressed to the brink. We’ve hardly met them and yet, within the vastness of the universe, they and the rest of Earth’s biota are our only known companions.
